==PAGE:136==
Shephela Project Pilot Study
Pilot study to MSc Thesis project
March 2017
Authors:
Tove Ekdahl s151134
Einar M Storebø s155265
==PAGE:137==

1

==PAGE:138==
Introduction
This pilot study and report consists of 4 separate studies (Figure 1) which was performed by
Einar Storebø and Tove Ekdahl in the period 02.02.17-31.03.17 as an initial part of our master
thesis projects at Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The results and observations from
these 4 studies gives important information in order to make decisions, plan and proceed our
individual master projects.
Pilot 1
Pilot 2
Pilot 3
Pilot 4
23.03
29.03
17.03
02.02
07.04
Figure 1: Timeline: start to end for all pilots, 2017.
The main project is to investigate temperature eﬀects and characterize an organic-rich car-
bonate rock from the Shephela basin in central Israel. The core material originates from 560
m depth in the Zoharim borehole and is named 83B.
From earlier work by El Zalaf (2016) it was noted that the stiﬀness of the material signiﬁ-
cantly change when heated from room temperature to 300¶C. From work by Kays (2016), it
was noted that the material becomes brittle and develops cracks along the bedding plane when
heated and cleaned with methanol. Both El Zalaf (2016) and Kays (2016) experienced great
diﬃculties in permeability measurements due to very low permeability. Based on these obser-
vations it was decided to run these Pilot studies prior to the master project. The diﬀerent pilot
studies are:
• Pilot 1: Identify the interesting temperature interval where stiﬀness of the material change
by stepwise heated oedometer test.
• Pilot 2: Investigate the eﬀect of cleaning the material with methanol on samples for
oedometer test. Method is similar to Pilot 1 but will be performed on a sample cleaned
with methanol to remove salt. Pilot 2 may also verify results from Pilot 1 concerning
temperature interval where the material stiﬀness change.
• Pilot 3: Investigate if a smaller sample height compared to El Zalaf (2016) and Kays (2016)
is possible to use in the PoroPerm machine when measuring Klinkenberg permeability,
using nitrogen gas.
• Pilot 4: Investigate if the order of sample preparation (cleaning with methanol and heat-
ing) has any visual eﬀect of the brittleness or development of cracks in the material.

==PAGE:140==
Pilot 1
Info:
By: Tove Ekdahl and Einar M Storebø
Duration: 02/02-2017 to 29/03-2017
Project: Shephela basin, Zoharim borehole
Sample:C1
Objective:
Previous research by El Zalaf (2016) has provided valuable information with respect to tem-
perature treatment of the organic rich carbonate from the Shephela basin and the Zoharim
borehole in particular. The research by El Zalaf (2016) was conducted on a core obtained from
a depth of 337m heated to the following temperatures: 25, 75, 125, and 300¶C.
The research showed an increasing stiﬀness of the rock with increasing temperature treat-
ment until 125¶C. Somewhere above 125¶C ther was a turning-point where stiddness dereased
signiﬁcantly to become much lower at 300¶C.
El Zalaf (2016)‘s results sheds light on a interesting property of this carbonate rock, however
still leaves a gap in the in the investigated temperature interval. The main objective of Pilot
1 is to outline a test temperature interval that includes the turning point temperatures as well
as provide new data points in the interval between 125 and 300¶C, where El Zalaf (2016) did
not produce any data.
Method:
By running a heated oedometer test program multiple times on the same core plug, each with
increased target temperature of the heater (see Figure 4), it is possible to track potential
variations of the rock stiﬀness properties as a function of temperature. Ultrasonic properties
is measured simultaneously. The test is performed on an untreated core plug (neither cleaned
or pre-heated), with an oedometer cell, where only axial strain is possible, surrounded by a
heating element.
Summary:
By performing a heated oedometer test and ultrasonic measurements on an untreated core
plug, its stiﬀness properties as a function of temperature were investigated. It was found that
the rock stiﬀness from the static oedometer modulus increase from ambient temperature up to
75¶C before it started decreasing again. At the same temperature point the dynamic modulus
where showing the opposite trend.
3
==PAGE:141==
Pilot 1
Procedure:
Test program and Sample information
The test is performed starting at ambient temperature running eighth cycles from 1 to 12 to
1 MPa, before increasing the temperature, iterating by 25C¶ at each stage using a strain-rate
based loading/unloading of 1000 micron/hr, see Figure 2. Given the load rate and number of
cycles, the total test time totals 184 hours. The sample information is presented in Table 2.
The loading where chosen to eighth cycles to investigate the presence of any hysteresis eﬀect
and plastic deformation. The endpoints of 1 and 12 MPa where chosen as previous research
show that this interval is in the elastic region of the sample used in this particular study (560m).
Studies by Bisnovat et al. (2015) indicates a UCS of approximately 53 MPa for this formation
at depth 560m. In the upper region (341m) of the depth interval the same study show a UCS
of 12.7 MPa substantiated by the studies of El Zalaf (2016) showing a onset pore collapse at
approximately 13.5 MPa
This experiment investigates the temperature eﬀect on elastic properties, ie the intention is
to stay in the elastic region and not induce any failure during testing.
The test was performed using a oedometer cell placed in a loadcell capable of exerting a
force of 50KN. The load pistons used includes both sound transducer and receiver enabling
ultra sonic measurements. For thermal treatment a heating element was placed over the oe-
dometer cell during the whole experiment using a user input controller for manual temperature
settings. The heating element temperature limit was 200 ¶C. To reduce the total test time the
temperature iteration was chosen to 25¶C, testing from ambient to 200¶C.
Note: All the equipment was calibrated prior to testing

Result:
The stress strain curves of the 8th and last load cycle for each temperature point are pre-
sented in Figure 3. The temperature in the legend in Figure 3 are presented as the measured
value inside the cell instead of target temperature on the controller. Further, Figure 4 shows
the calculated static oedometer modulus and dynamic P-wave modulus at each temperature
point.
The obtained oedometer odulus results shows the same initial increase in strength from
ambient temperature to 75¶C as in the study by El Zalaf (2016), whereas the turning point in
this study comes at an earlier stage.

Recommendation:

==PAGE:144==
Pilot 2
Info:
By: Tove Ekdahl and Einar M Storebø
Duration: 02/02-2017 to 07/04-2017
Project: Shaphela basin, Zoharim borehole
Sample:C2
Note: Pilot 2 is identical to Pilot 1 with the exception that sample C2 is cleaned with methanol.
Objective:
Conﬁrm results from Pilot 1 and observe the eﬀect of cleaning with methanol.
Method:
Same as Pilot 1 in addition to cleaning the sample with methanol prior to the testing.
Summary:
By performing a heated oedometer test and ultrasonic measurements on a methanol cleaned
sample, its stiﬀness properties as a function of temperature were investigated. This experiment
is a repetition of Pilot 1 and the aim was to supplement and conﬁrm the results from Pilot 1.
The oedometer modulus results from Pilot 2 was not not similar to Pilot 1, but the P-wave
modulus show similar trend and values as for Pilot 1. The result do indicate however, that
the mechanical properties of the rock change with heating temperature. Based on the results
of Pilot 1 and 2 the following temperatures were recommended for further investigation in the
master projects: 25 (reference temperature), 75, 125, 150 and 175¶C.
7
==PAGE:145==
Pilot 2
Procedure:
Pilot 2 followed the exact same procedure and equipment as Pilot 1, (see procedure Pilot
1) with the exception of methanol cleaning.
Methanol Cleaning
Cleaning in methanol was performed to remove salts in the sample plug. The dry sample was
placed in a soxhlet apparatus, see Figure 5, where the methanol is continuously distilled so that
the sample is soaked in clean methanol at all times, while the salt is removed. The methanol
is continuously tested during the cleaning process using AgNO3. If Cl ions are present there
will be a visual precipitation of AgCl when AgNO3 is mixed with some of the methanol used
for cleaning. The sample is assumed to be cleaned from salts if no precipitation is observed.
Figure 5: Soxhlet apparatus for cleaning samples.
8
==PAGE:146==
Result:
During the methanol cleaning process sample C2 developed a horizontal crack, see Figure
6.
Figure 6: Sample C2 after methanol cleaning with a resulting horizontal crack from the process.
The stress strain curves for all temperatures is shown in Figure 7.
The initial interval
from approximately 1-7 MPa shows a signiﬁcantly steeper slope than at stress higher than 7
MPa, especially for the curves of the lower temperatures. This can be interpreted as the crack
(Figure 6) is closing, however it could also be an eﬀect of the cleaning process itself or removing
of salts. Based on this observation both static and dynamic moduli where interpreted at the
stress interval above 8 MPa during the loading phase.
Figure 7: Stress strain curves from last cycle at each temperature for Pilot 1
9
==PAGE:147==
The static and dynamic moduli from both Pilot 1 and 2 are shown i Figure 8. The dynamic
moduli from both experiments are showing a similar trend and values, while the static moduli
diﬀer signiﬁcantly at lower temperatures.

Recommendation:
Looking at the data from Pilot 1 and 2 it is recommended to select temperatures for fur-
ther investigation such as the turning point of the moduli are covered and in this case the
following temperatures: 25, 75, 125, 150 and 175¶C.
Note: The recommended temperature interval is based on a the outcome of the two pilots,
however the results include uncertainty and therefore the recommended interval is not assured
to reveal all signiﬁcant changes in the mechanical properties with respect to temperature. This
study aims to observe changes, not quantify.

==PAGE:148==
Pilot 3
Info:
By: Tove Ekdahl and Einar M Storebø
Duration: 02/02-2017 to 17/03-2017
Project: Shephela basin, Zoharim borehole
Sample:D1 and D3
Objective:
Based on observations from earlier work by El Zalaf (2016) and Kays (2016) on the material it
is known that the very low permeability causes measurement issues when using the PoroPerm
apparatus (with Nitrogen gas). Previous tests on sample height 7cm failed to measure perme-
ability (El Zalaf, 2016). Previous tests on sample height 2cm was possible but diﬃcult and
very time-consuming since the very low permeability causes a slow rate in pressure drop over
the sample. Hence a very long waiting time is required to reach steady state conditions and be
able to make a measurement which fulﬁlls the Darcy recommendation of laminar ﬂow. Many
of the samples being both cleaned with methanol and heated up to 300¶C cracked at several
places during the permeability measurements.
Prior to Pilot 3, a new (longer) component was produced to allow keeping a sample plug of
height less than 2 cm in place in the PoroPerm apparatus. The objective of Pilot 3 was to
investigate the functionality using the new component and if permeability measurement was
possible on a sample plug of height less than 2 cm.
Method:
By measuring permeability using the PoroPerm apparatus with the new apparatus component
it is possible to verify the functionality of the new component. By measuring the permeability
of samples with height 1.2 cm, exposed to diﬀerent pre-treatments, it is possible to see if the
measurement is possible and if the samples are aﬀected diﬀerently. The diﬀerent pre-treatments
are: no pre-treatment, cleaned with methanol to remove salt and heated to 300¶C until reaching
constant weight.
Summary:
By measuring permeability of sample plugs with height 1.2 cm with the PoroPerm apparatus
using the new component, the quality of the new component and the possible measurement
issues of the low permeability samples were investigated. The results show that the new com-
ponent works ﬁne and that measurements on samples with small height (< 2cm) is possible
but still very time consuming. Permeability measurements by PoroPerm of this organic rich
carbonate is diﬃcult and time-consuming. A sample that developed a visible crack when heated
to 300¶C split into two pieces during the permeability measurement.
11
==PAGE:149==
Pilot 3
Procedure:
• Produce a new longer component for keeping the sample plug in right position inside the
PoroPerm apparatus core holder.
• Cut 2 test samples of height 1.2 cm and dry the test samples in room temperature until
constant weight.
• Perform a 5 point gas permeability measurement on a dry, non-treated sample and esti-
mate liquid permeability.
• Clean one test sample in methanol to remove salt. Heat the other test sample in 300¶C
until constant weight.
• Perform a 5 point gas permeability measurement on the two dry, pre-treated samples and
estimate liquid permeability.
• Evaluate the results.
Results:
The results of the diﬀerent sets of permeability measurements are presented in Table 3. A
negative liquid permeability indicates a failure and the results do not reﬂect the reality other
than the material has very low permeability. However, the measurements do show that it is
possible to make measurements but it is diﬃcult to get good measurements.
Figure 10 shows images of a crack developed in sample D3 during heating to 300¶C. Permeabil-
ity measurements by PoroPerm was then performed on the heated sample which caused full
development of the crack and the sample was split in two pieces.

Recommendation:
Due to the high risk of splitting fragile samples when measuring permeability by PoroPerm,
consider a diﬀerent method to estimate permeability or use "spare" samples which may aﬀord
the risk of being damaged. Evaluate if the quality of the obtained test results are worth the
great amount of time needed to perform the permeability measurements.

==PAGE:151==
Pilot 4
Info:
By: Tove Ekdahl and Einar M Storebø
Duration: 02/02-2017 to 23/03-2017
Project: Shephela basin, Zoharim borehole
Sample:C0 and D0.1
Objective:
Based on observations from earlier work by Kays (2016), core samples become fragile and the
risk of developing cracks when cleaned with methanol and toluene is high. The problem is
worse for samples heated to high temperatures (200¶C and 300¶C). In the work by Kays (2016)
the samples were ﬁrst heated and then cleaned. The objective of Pilot 4 is to investigate if the
order of cleaning and heating makes any diﬀerence in developing cracks in the core material.
Method:
By heating and cleaning 2 samples in diﬀerent orders and inspect the samples visually after each
treatment, it is possible to recommend a treatment order if the samples diﬀer signiﬁcantly. If
no visible diﬀerence is observed between the samples, it is assumed that the order of treatment
is not of importance regarding the risk of developing cracks.
Summary:
Samples are cleaned and heated in diﬀerent order and visually inspected to make observations
regarding development of cracks in the samples. The objective is to investigate if the order of
treatment matters to the risk of developing cracks. It was observed that some samples develop
cracks when cleaned and some when heated and it cannot be concluded that the order of
treatment matters. It seems the risk of developing cracks is sample speciﬁc and not dependent
on the order of treatment.
14
==PAGE:152==
Pilot 4
Procedure:
• Two side trimming samples (C0 and D0.1) of equal quality and origin in the core were
selected for the study.
• C0 was ﬁrst cleaned in methanol and then heated to 300¶C. D0.1 was ﬁrst heated to
300¶C and then cleaned in methanol.
• Both samples were inspected visually after each treatment and observations noted.
Results:
Sample C0 did not crack when cleaned with methanol but developed a thin horizontal crack
when heated to 300¶C, see Figure 11 a).
Sample D0.1 did not develop crack(s) from heating, nor from cleaning in methanol, see Figure
11 b).
Other observations: sample C2 (non-heated) which was cleaned together with C0, developed
a horizontal crack when cleaned, see Figure 11 c). Sample D1 was also cleaned in methanol
at the same time and did not develop cracks, see Figure 11 d). Sample D3 (non-cleaned) was
heated to 300¶C together with D0.1 and developed a sub-horizontal crack, see Figure 10.
Together, all observations cannot support neither decline that the order of cleaning and heating
aﬀects the development of cracks in the samples. These observations rather indicates that the
risk of developing cracks is sample speciﬁc and hence indicating that the core (which is clearly
laminated) is heterogeneous.

when heated to 300¶C.
(b) D0.1, not cracked by
heating to 300¶C or clean-

Figure 11: Observations of cracks on some samples.

==PAGE:153==
Recommendation:
The order of cleaning and heating, concerning the risk of developing cracks in the samples,
is assumed to be of no importance.
